#The following list of PDB ids was generated based on the query of the RCSB PDB in the file:
#  query_rcsb_protein_training_set.json
#
#Note that query in query_rcsb_protein_training_set.json is set up to return "polymer entity" ids...
#These are the PDB id followed by an underscore and the molecule number for the protein in that PDB.
#So it could be PDBid_3 for the third molecule in a PDB entry.
#This is set up so that each molecule can be compared against other molecules by sequence.
#Then only the highest resolution of that protein sequence is kept (based on a 50% sequence similarity cutoff).
#That means that there may be multiple of the same PDB id in the returned results.
#But for training, we only want to include full complexes if that is what was measured experimentally.
#That's because removing one chain would leave an interface that SHOULD have protein atoms as context.
#Leaving out a binding partner means the training algorithm will assume that interface is solvent-exposed.
#That is not what we want, but so we want to work with only entire PDB structures as much as possible.
#For that reason, the query only considers complexes with fewer than 10 proteins and less than 5,000 atoms.
#And only protein molecules (could include small-molecule ligands, but these will be removed for training).
#
#To take the list of all unique "polymer entity" ids and get unique PDB ids with python:
#  import json
#  import numpy as np
#  f = open('results.json', 'r')
#  data = json.load(f)
#  f.close()
#  pdb_ids = [a[:4] for a in data['result_set']]
#  unique_ids = set(pdb_ids)
#  unique_ids = np.array(list(unique_ids))
#  np.savetxt('unique_pdbids.txt', unique_ids, fmt='%s')
#
